فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: البعد المجرد وهو الفضاء غير قابل للحركة، والبعد الذي يكون في المادة، وهو بعد الجسم يكون قابلا للحركة لأجل أن الشرط في كون البعد قابلاً للحركة، كونه في المادة؟ نقول: هذا السؤال باطل من وجهين: الأول: سنقيم الدلالة على أن البعد يمتنع أن يقال: إنه حال في محل، وأنه صفة لشيء آخر. والثاني: وهو أنا نقول: هب أن البعد يعقل كونه حالاً في محل، إلا أنا نقول: ذلك المحل من حيث إنه هو، إما أن يكون شاغلاً للحيز والجهة وإما أن لا يكون كذلك، بل يكون من الموجودات المجردة عن الوضع والإشارة. والأول باطل لأنه يقتضي أن يكون محل البعد بعداً، فإن افتقر البعد إلى محل، فليفتقر ذلك المحل لكونه بعداً إلى محل آخر، ولزم التسلسل، وهو محال. وأما الثاني فهو باطل أيضاً، لأن كل موجود يكون مجرداً عن الإشارة والحيز والجهة، فإنه يمتنع عليه الانتقال والحركة. لأن الانتقال والحركة عبارة عن الحصول في حيز، بعد أن كان حاصلاً في حيز آخر، وهذا إنما يعقل في حق الشيء الذي يصح عليه كونه حاصلاً في الحيز والجهة، أما الشيء الذي يمتنع كونه حاصلاً في الحيز والجهة، فإنه يمتنع كونه قابلاً للحركة. فيثبت: أن هذه المادة غير قابلة للحركة أصلاً. وإذا كان كذلك، فحينئذ يمتنع أن يقال: إن كون البعد حالاً في المادة، شرط لكون البعد قابلاً للحركة، لأن حلول البعد في المادة معناه: أنه قارنه شيء لا يقبل الحركة، ومقارنة مثل هذا الشيء، يجب أن يكون مانعاً من الحركة، ولا يعقل كونه شرطاً لصحة الحركة. فقد زال هذا السؤال.
If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say: the abstract dimension, which is space, is not susceptible to motion, and the dimension that is in matter, which is the dimension of a body, is susceptible to motion because the condition for a dimension's susceptibility to motion is its being in matter? We say: This question is false in two respects. First: we will establish the proof that dimension cannot be said to be an accident in a subject, nor a quality of something else. Second: we say: Suppose that dimension is intelligible as an accident in a subject; but we say: that subject, insofar as it is itself, is either occupying space and direction, or it is not, but rather is among beings abstracted from position and indication. The first is false because it requires that the subject of dimension be a dimension; for if dimension needs a subject, then let that subject, because it is a dimension, need another subject, and an infinite regress follows, which is impossible. As for the second, it is also false, because every being that is abstracted from indication, space, and direction is not susceptible to motion—transition and motion are defined as obtaining in a space after having been in another space, and this is only intelligible for something that can be located in space and direction; something that cannot be located in space and direction cannot be susceptible to motion. Thus it is established that this matter is not susceptible to motion at all. And if so, then it is impossible to say that the dimension's being in matter is a condition for the dimension's susceptibility to motion, because the inherence of dimension in matter means that it is accompanied by something that does not accept motion, and the accompaniment of such a thing must prevent motion, and cannot be a condition for the validity of motion. So this objection disappears.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that abstract dimension (space) is immobile, but material dimension (of bodies) is mobile because it is in matter. Al-Razi refutes this in two ways. First, he will prove that dimension cannot be an accident inhering in a subject at all. Second, even if it could, the subject of dimension must be either spatial or non-spatial. If spatial, the subject itself would need a subject (infinite regress). If non-spatial, it would be abstract and thus immobile, making matter itself immobile. Hence the objection fails.

Key Terms
بعد مجرد (abstract dimension)
بعد في المادة (dimension in matter)
حلول (inherence)
محل (subject)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
حركة (motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dimension can be in matter as an accident, and that makes it movable.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even if it were, the subject (matter) is either spatial causing regress, or non-spatial causing immobility, so the condition fails.

Checkpoint
How many reasons does al-Razi give for the invalidity of the objection?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes the objection by showing that the condition of inherence in matter is either impossible or leads to immobility, so the objection collapses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi exposes a contradiction in an intuitive assumption about matter and motion. What comfortable assumption about your own faith or worldview might be logically flawed? Will you take time to examine it, even if it feels unsettling?
?
The argument hinges on the principle of infinite regress—an impossibility that Al-Razi uses to refute an objection. When have you accepted a justification without following its logical implications to the end? How can you train yourself to think more rigorously about matters of belief?
?
Al-Razi shows that even if we grant a suspect premise (dimension as accident), the conclusion still fails. Are there beliefs you hold that you have only defended with one weak argument? Would you be willing to test them from multiple angles, even if it means admitting error?

Al-Razi's Fourth Argument: Divisibility of Space
Al-Razi's fourth argument: divisibility of space leads to a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's fourth argument against space as a dimension, which uses the divisibility of space to derive a dilemma between infinite regress and compositeness.

Core Idea
Space's divisibility forces a choice: parts equal in essence cause regress; unequal parts make space composite.

Al-Razi presents a refined argument to challenge the concept of space as a dimension. He begins with a methodological note, then moves into the core of the argument: since space is divisible, its parts must be either homogeneous or heterogeneous—both leading to problematic conclusions. This lesson walks you through that reasoning, even though the text cuts off just as it reaches the upshot.

Guided Path
1
Read the methodological preface
Notice that Al-Razi frames this argument as supporting Aristotle and opposing Plato, and he states two key premises: dimensions are equal in essence, and what holds for one holds for its like.
This sets the stage for the logical structure that follows.
2
Understand the division of space
Al-Razi asserts that space is divisible by mental division (by hypothesis and indication). He gives an example: 'from here to there is a cubit.' This is not physical cutting but conceptual partitioning.
The division is solely in the mind, not in reality.
3
Examine the two possibilities
The parts are either equal in complete essence or not. The text then explores each branch. Follow the reasoning for the first branch (homogeneous) and then the second (heterogeneous).
The argument is a dilemma: both options lead to absurdities.
4
Identify the consequences
If parts are homogeneous, one part can move to another's location, implying space has a place (infinite regress). If heterogeneous, space is composite, requiring decomposition into simples (implying space is not fundamental).
The text ends before stating the final conclusion, but the problems are clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذه الحجة كلام حسن رتبناه نحن للقوم نصرة لمذهب أرسطاطاليس، وطعناً في قول أفلاطون، إلا أنها مبنية أيضاً على: أن الأبعاد متساوية في تمام الماهية، وأن كل ما صح على الشيء، فإنه يصح أيضاً على مثله. والله أعلم. للحجة الرابعة: هذا البعد المسمى بالفضاء والمكان والحيز، لا شك أنه يكون قابلا للقسمة، بحسب الفرض والإشارة. فإنا نقول: من ههنا إلى هناك ذراع، ثم من هناك إلى موضع آخر ذراع آخر. ولا معنى للقسمة بحسب الفرض والإشارة إلا ذلك. إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: هذه الأجزاء المفترضة في هذا البعد بحسب الإشارة، إما أن تكون متساوية في تمام الماهية، وإما أن لا تكون كذلك. فإن كان الأول وجب أن يصح على كل واحد منهما ما صح على الآخر، وإذا كان كذلك فالجزء الذي حصل فوق، وجب صحة حصوله تحت، وبالعكس. وذلك يوجب صحة الحركة والانتقال عليها، وكل ما يصح عليه الحركة والانتقال، فله مكان، فللمكان مكان. وحينئذ تعود المحالات المذكورة. وأما إن كانت تلك الأجزاء المفترضة فيها غير متساوية في تمام الماهية، فحئنئذ يكون هذا الفضاء مركباً من أجزاء مختلفة بحسب الماهية فيكون مركباً، وكل مركب فإنه ينتهي تحليل تركيبه إلى البسائط،
And know that this argument is a good speech we have arranged in support of the school of Aristotle and in refutation of Plato's statement, but it is also based on: that dimensions are equal in complete essence, and that whatever holds for a thing also holds for its like. And God knows best. For the fourth argument: This dimension called space, place, and void is undoubtedly divisible by hypothetically and by indication. We say: from here to there is a cubit, then from there to another location is another cubit. There is no meaning to division by hypothesis and indication except that. When this is established, we say: these hypothetical parts in this dimension by indication are either equal in complete essence or not. If the first, then whatever holds for one must hold for the other; if so, the part that is above must be capable of being below, and vice versa. This necessitates motion and transference upon them, and everything that allows motion and transference has a place, so place would have a place. Then the aforementioned impossibilities return. And if those hypothetical parts in it are not equal in complete essence, then this space would be composed of parts differing in essence, and thus it would be composite; and every composite, its decomposition ultimately leads to simples,
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage contains a methodological note and the beginning of the fourth argument. Al-Razi notes that the previous argument (likely the one in lesson 1) is a good one he constructed to support Aristotle and refute Plato, but it relies on the premise that dimensions are homogeneous (equal in essence) and that what holds for one holds for its like. Then he introduces the fourth argument: space is divisible by mental division. The parts are either homogeneous or heterogeneous. If homogeneous, then a part can move to another part's location, implying space has a place (infinite regress). If heterogeneous, space is composite and composed of simpler elements, implying it is not simple. The argument is cut off at 'simples,' but the intended conclusion is that both possibilities are problematic.

القسمة
الفرض والإشارة
الماهية
مركب
بسائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division of space in the argument is physical cutting, not conceptual.
Correction: Al-Razi explicitly states that the division is 'by hypothesis and indication' (mental), not actual physical division.

Checkpoint
What two possibilities does al-Razi consider regarding the parts of space in his fourth argument?
A) Equal or unequal in motion
B) Equal or unequal in essence
C) Material or immaterial
D) Finite or infinite

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's fourth argument forces a dilemma: space's divisibility leads either to an infinite regress (if parts are homogeneous) or to space being composite and non-fundamental (if parts are heterogeneous).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look around at the space you occupy every day—your room, your path to the mosque—do you ever question whether that space is truly simple? If a great thinker like Al-Razi could not resolve its nature without contradictions, how confidently can you claim to understand the stage upon which your entire life plays out? Will you let this uncertainty humble your intellectual pride before the Creator of space itself?
?
Al-Razi’s argument forces you to choose between two uncomfortable options: either space leads to an infinite regress or it is made of parts. Both challenge your common-sense picture. When you plan your day with distances and places, you assume space is a stable backdrop. After this lesson, can you still take that stability for granted? Today, crucially, how will you remind yourself that even the most basic assumptions about the world are signs pointing to the infinite power of Allah?

The Three False Explanations for a Dimension's Shape
Sanusi refutes three possible causes for a dimension's shape.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the cause of a dimension's specific shape cannot be its own nature, an inherent quality, or a separate entity.

Core Idea
Shapes cannot arise from dimension itself, its attributes, or external agents alone.

This lesson unpacks the first part of Sanusi's argument, where he systematically eliminates three possible causes for why a dimension has a particular shape. By ruling out these alternatives, he forces the conclusion that the true cause must be something else—a material substrate. We will follow his logical refutations step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the opened Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The author lists three possible determinants of shape: (1) the nature of dimension itself, (2) something inhering in the dimension, (3) something separate from the dimension. Find these in the text.
3
Follow the refutation of each
For each possibility, note the specific reason it fails. The first leads to part-equal-whole absurdity; the second either repeats the first or becomes a separate entity problem; the third leads to arbitrary specification or infinite regress.
This is a typical kalam argument by exhaustively eliminating alternatives.
4
Understand the conclusion
After refuting all three, the author states that the only remaining possibility is that the determinant is a substrate (mahall) for the dimension. That substrate is matter.
This conclusion sets the stage for the next lesson on how matter gives specific shape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأسرها باطلة . إلا قولنا : المقتضى لذلك الشكل المعين ما يكون محلاً لذلك البعد . فنقول : إنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون المقتضى لذلك الشكل ، هو نفس طبيعة البعد والمقدار . وذلك لأن الجزء [ المفروض من ] ذلك البعد ، مساو للكل في الطبيعة والماهية فيلزم أن يكون شكل الجزء مساوياً لشكل الكل وذلك باطل . وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون المقتضى لذلك الشكل أمراً حالاً في ذلك البعد . وذلك لأن ذلك الحال إن كان من لوازم الطبيعة المقدارية ، عاد ما ذكرناه من أنه يلزم كون الجزء مساوياً للكل في الشكل ، وإن لم يكن من لوازمها ، امتنع كونها علة للشكل الذي هو من لوازمها ، لأن المفارق يمتنع كونه علة للصفة اللازمة . وإنما قلنا : إن المقتضى لذلك الشكل يمتنع أن يكون أمراً مغايراً لنفس المقدار ، ولما يكون حالاً فيه ولما يكون محلاً له . وذلك لأن المباين إما أن تكون نسبته إلى هذا البعد المسمى بالفضاء ، وإلى جميع أجزائه على السوية ، وإما أن لا يكون . فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يكون اختصاص هذا البعد ، بهذا الشكل المعين دون سائر الأبعاد رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر من غير مرجح ، وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ اختصاص هذا البعد المسمى بالفضاء . بما لأجله صار أولى بقبول هذا الشكل من ذلك المفارق ؟ إن كان لاجل مفارق آخر عاد الكلام فيه . ولزم التسلسل . وإن كان لا لهذا السبب ، فحينئذ يعود الأمر إلى أن اختصاصه بتلك الأولوية ، إما أن يكون لذاته، أو لما يكون حالا فيه ، أو لما يكون محلا له ، أو للمباين. ولما بطلت هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ، ثبت أن اختصاص ذلك البعد المخصوص ، بذلك الشكل المخصوص ، إنما كان لأجل ما يكون محلاً له ، ومادة له . وهو المطلوب. فإن قالوا : فمادة الكل مساوية لمادة الجزء فيعود الاشتراك المذكور . فنقول في الجواب : إن ذلك البعد لما كان حالاً في تلك المادة ، وكانت تلك المادة موصوفة بصفات ، لأجلها كانت مستعدة لقبول ذلك الشكل من واهب الصور، لا جرم حصل ذلك الشكل دون سائر الأشكال. فيثبت بمجموع ما ذكرنا : أن كل بعد فهو متناهي وكل متناهي مشكل ، وكل متشكل فإنما حصل له شكله المعين بسبب المادة ، ينتج أن كل بعد فإنه لا يوجد إلا إذا كان حاصلا في المادة . وإذا ثبت هذا كان القول بوجود البعد المجرد محالاً وذلك هو المطلوب .
All of them are false. Except for our statement: the determinant of that particular shape is what is a substrate for that dimension. So we say: We only say that it is not permissible for the determinant of that shape to be the very nature of dimension and magnitude. That is because the [supposed] part of that dimension is equal to the whole in nature and essence, so the shape of the part would necessarily be equal to the shape of the whole, and that is false. And we only say that it is not permissible for the determinant of that shape to be something inhering in that dimension. That is because if that inherent thing is among the necessary concomitants of the quantitative nature, then what we mentioned returns—that the part would necessarily be equal to the whole in shape; and if it is not among its concomitants, then it is impossible for it to be a cause of the shape which is one of its concomitants, because a separate thing cannot be a cause of a necessary attribute. And we only say that it is impossible for the determinant of that shape to be something distinct from the magnitude itself, from what is inhering in it, and from what is a substrate for it. That is because the separate [entity] either has a relation to this dimension called 'space' and to all its parts equally, or it does not. If the first, then the specification of this dimension with this particular shape to the exclusion of other dimensions would be a preponderance of one side of the possible over another without a preponderator, which is absurd. If the second, then the specification of this dimension called 'space' is due to something by which it becomes more worthy of accepting this shape than that separate [entity]? If it is because of another separate, then the same discussion applies and an infinite regress follows. If it is not for this reason, then the matter returns to the fact that its specification with that priority is either by its essence, or by something inhering in it, or by something that is a substrate for it, or by something separate. Since these three divisions are false, it is established that the specification of that particular dimension with that particular shape is only due to what is a substrate for it and matter for it. And that is what is desired. If they say: the matter of the whole is equal to the matter of the part, so the aforementioned commonality returns. We say in reply: since that dimension is inhering in that matter, and that matter is qualified by attributes due to which it is prepared to accept that shape from the Giver of Forms, inevitably that shape occurs to the exclusion of other shapes. So it is established by the totality of what we have mentioned: that every dimension is finite, and every finite thing is shaped, and every shaped thing only obtains its particular shape due to matter. This yields that every dimension only exists if it is subsisting in matter. And if this is established, then the statement that an abstract dimension exists is impossible, and that is what is desired.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof that every dimension (al-bu'd) must inhere in matter (madda) and cannot exist independently. The author begins by stating that all alternative explanations for the cause of a dimension's specific shape are false, except the one that identifies the cause as something that is a substrate (mahall) for that dimension. He then systematically refutes three possibilities: (1) The cause is the nature of dimension itself: if so, a part would have the same shape as the whole, which is false. (2) The cause is something inhering in the dimension: if that inhering thing is a necessary concomitant of the quantitative nature, the same problem arises; if not, it cannot cause a necessary concomitant. (3) The cause is something separate from the dimension: if its relation to all parts is equal, then specification of shape would be arbitrary; if not equal, an infinite regress or a return to the previous possibilities occurs. Since these three are impossible, the only remaining cause is a material substrate. The author then addresses an objection: if the whole and part share the same matter, why aren't shapes identical? The answer is that the matter possesses specific dispositions (sifat) that make it receptive to a particular shape from the Giver of Forms (wahib al-suwar). The conclusion is that every dimension is finite, shaped, and exists only in matter, so a purely abstract dimension is impossible.

Key Terms
بعد (dimension)
شكل (shape)
محل (substrate)
طبيعة مقدارية (quantitative nature)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility for the cause of a dimension's shape that the author refutes?
A) The matter that is the substrate
B) The nature of the dimension itself
C) Something separate from the dimension
D) Something inhering in the dimension

Key Takeaway
The shape of a dimension cannot be caused by its own nature, its inherent attributes, or an external separate entity; it must be caused by a material substrate.

What would change if you did the latter?'}

The Material Substrate as the True Cause of Shape
Sanusi refutes an objection and concludes that dimension requires matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how matter determines a dimension's specific shape through its dispositions and the role of the Giver of Forms, and how the author answers the objection about shared matter.

Core Idea
Matter gives shape through dispositions and divine causation.

Having eliminated alternative explanations, the author now defends the conclusion that matter is the cause of a dimension's shape. He then addresses an objection: if matter is shared by the whole and its parts, why aren't shapes identical? His answer invokes the specific dispositions of matter and the Giver of Forms. This final part of the passage seals the proof that no dimension can exist without matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Understand the objection: if matter is common to whole and part, then shapes should be the same. The author refutes this by noting that matter has specific dispositions.
2
Grasp the role of dispositions and the Giver of Forms
The author explains that matter is prepared to accept a particular shape from the Giver of Forms due to its attributes (sifat). This explains why shapes differ despite shared matter.
This shows compatibility with an external efficient cause (wahib al-suwar).
3
Trace the final syllogism
The author summarizes: every dimension is finite, every finite thing is shaped, every shaped thing gets its shape from matter. Therefore, every dimension exists only in matter.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
The conclusion that an abstract dimension (al-bu'd al-mujarrad) is impossible is a key metaphysical point against atomism or vacuum theories.
This lesson completes the proof begun in the previous one.

بأسرها باطلة . إلا قولنا : المقتضى لذلك الشكل المعين ما يكون محلاً لذلك البعد . فنقول : إنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون المقتضى لذلك الشكل ، هو نفس طبيعة البعد والمقدار . وذلك لأن الجزء [ المفروض من ] ذلك البعد ، مساو للكل في الطبيعة والماهية فيلزم أن يكون شكل الجزء مساوياً لشكل الكل وذلك باطل . وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون المقتضى لذلك الشكل أمراً حالاً في ذلك البعد . وذلك لأن ذلك الحال إن كان من لوازم الطبيعة المقدارية ، عاد ما ذكرناه من أنه يلزم كون الجزء مساوياً للكل في الشكل ، وإن لم يكن من لوازمها ، امتنع كونها علة للشكل الذي هو من لوازمها ، لأن المفارق يمتنع كونه علة للصفة اللازمة . وإنما قلنا : إن المقتضى لذلك الشكل يمتنع أن يكون أمراً مغايراً لنفس المقدار ، ولما يكون حالاً فيه ولما يكون محلاً له . وذلك لأن المباين إما أن تكون نسبته إلى هذا البعد المسمى بالفضاء ، وإلى جميع أجزائه على السوية ، وإما أن لا يكون . فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يكون اختصاص هذا البعد ، بهذا الشكل المعين دون سائر الأبعاد رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر من غير مرجح ، وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ اختصاص هذا البعد المسمى بالفضاء . بما لأجله صار أولى بقبول هذا الشكل من ذلك المفارق ؟ إن كان لاجل مفارق آخر عاد الكلام فيه . ولزم التسلسل . وإن كان لا لهذا السبب ، فحينئذ يعود الأمر إلى أن اختصاصه بتلك الأولوية ، إما أن يكون لذاته، أو لما يكون حالا فيه ، أو لما يكون محلا له ، أو للمباين. ولما بطلت هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ، ثبت أن اختصاص ذلك البعد المخصوص ، بذلك الشكل المخصوص ، إنما كان لأجل ما يكون محلاً له ، ومادة له . وهو المطلوب. فإن قالوا : فمادة الكل مساوية لمادة الجزء فيعود الاشتراك المذكور . فنقول في الجواب : إن ذلك البعد لما كان حالاً في تلك المادة ، وكانت تلك المادة موصوفة بصفات ، لأجلها كانت مستعدة لقبول ذلك الشكل من واهب الصور، لا جرم حصل ذلك الشكل دون سائر الأشكال. فيثبت بمجموع ما ذكرنا : أن كل بعد فهو متناهي وكل متناهي مشكل ، وكل متشكل فإنما حصل له شكله المعين بسبب المادة ، ينتج أن كل بعد فإنه لا يوجد إلا إذا كان حاصلا في المادة . وإذا ثبت هذا كان القول بوجود البعد المجرد محالاً وذلك هو المطلوب .
All of them are false. Except for our statement: the determinant of that particular shape is what is a substrate for that dimension. So we say: We only say that it is not permissible for the determinant of that shape to be the very nature of dimension and magnitude. That is because the [supposed] part of that dimension is equal to the whole in nature and essence, so the shape of the part would necessarily be equal to the shape of the whole, and that is false. And we only say that it is not permissible for the determinant of that shape to be something inhering in that dimension. That is because if that inherent thing is among the necessary concomitants of the quantitative nature, then what we mentioned returns—that the part would necessarily be equal to the whole in shape; and if it is not among its concomitants, then it is impossible for it to be a cause of the shape which is one of its concomitants, because a separate thing cannot be a cause of a necessary attribute. And we only say that it is impossible for the determinant of that shape to be something distinct from the magnitude itself, from what is inhering in it, and from what is a substrate for it. That is because the separate [entity] either has a relation to this dimension called 'space' and to all its parts equally, or it does not. If the first, then the specification of this dimension with this particular shape to the exclusion of other dimensions would be a preponderance of one side of the possible over another without a preponderator, which is absurd. If the second, then the specification of this dimension called 'space' is due to something by which it becomes more worthy of accepting this shape than that separate [entity]? If it is because of another separate, then the same discussion applies and an infinite regress follows. If it is not for this reason, then the matter returns to the fact that its specification with that priority is either by its essence, or by something inhering in it, or by something that is a substrate for it, or by something separate. Since these three divisions are false, it is established that the specification of that particular dimension with that particular shape is only due to what is a substrate for it and matter for it. And that is what is desired. If they say: the matter of the whole is equal to the matter of the part, so the aforementioned commonality returns. We say in reply: since that dimension is inhering in that matter, and that matter is qualified by attributes due to which it is prepared to accept that shape from the Giver of Forms, inevitably that shape occurs to the exclusion of other shapes. So it is established by the totality of what we have mentioned: that every dimension is finite, and every finite thing is shaped, and every shaped thing only obtains its particular shape due to matter. This yields that every dimension only exists if it is subsisting in matter. And if this is established, then the statement that an abstract dimension exists is impossible, and that is what is desired.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof that every dimension (al-bu'd) must inhere in matter (madda) and cannot exist independently. The author begins by stating that all alternative explanations for the cause of a dimension's specific shape are false, except the one that identifies the cause as something that is a substrate (mahall) for that dimension. He then systematically refutes three possibilities: (1) The cause is the nature of dimension itself: if so, a part would have the same shape as the whole, which is false. (2) The cause is something inhering in the dimension: if that inhering thing is a necessary concomitant of the quantitative nature, the same problem arises; if not, it cannot cause a necessary concomitant. (3) The cause is something separate from the dimension: if its relation to all parts is equal, then specification of shape would be arbitrary; if not equal, an infinite regress or a return to the previous possibilities occurs. Since these three are impossible, the only remaining cause is a material substrate. The author then addresses an objection: if the whole and part share the same matter, why aren't shapes identical? The answer is that the matter possesses specific dispositions (sifat) that make it receptive to a particular shape from the Giver of Forms (wahib al-suwar). The conclusion is that every dimension is finite, shaped, and exists only in matter, so a purely abstract dimension is impossible.

Key Terms
مادة (matter)
واهب الصور (Giver of Forms)
صفات (attributes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what causes the difference in shape between the whole and its parts despite shared matter?
A) The matter is different in each part
B) The matter has different dispositions (sifat) that affect receptivity
C) The Giver of Forms arbitrarily chooses different shapes
D) The dimension's own nature determines the shape

Key Takeaway
The diversity of shapes despite shared matter is explained by material dispositions and the Giver of Forms, leading to the conclusion that all dimensions depend on matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that every shape you see—including the form of your own body—is given directly by the Giver of Forms. Does your daily life reflect the belief that your physical existence is a constant gift from God, or do you behave as if your body is self-sufficient? What one action can you take today to acknowledge that your very shape is sustained by Him?
?
If abstract dimensions (like empty space) are impossible, then there is no part of the universe that is independent of matter and, by extension, independent of God's creative act. Where in your thinking have you assumed that some part of reality—your career, your relationships, your health—exists on its own, separate from God? Will you repent of that assumption and view every dimension of your life as contingent upon Him?
?
The proof showed that matter's preparedness for shape comes from its attributes, but the actual shape is bestowed from outside. What 'shape' (character, habits, or state) are you trying to produce in yourself by mere personal effort, without relying on the Giver of Forms? How can you actively depend on God to grant you the form He intends for you today?

Objection that dimension could be independent of matter
Objection that dimension could be self-subsisting with its own attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objector argues that a dimension could be self-subsisting with its own attributes, and why this challenges the author's claim that every dimension must inhere in matter.

Core Idea
The objector claims that quantitative nature alone can have attributes to determine shape without matter.

In this passage, a critic raises a technical objection against the claim that every dimension must inhere in matter. Instead of accepting the author's reasoning, the critic proposes a scenario where the quantitative nature itself possesses attributes that determine shape, thereby dispensing with a material substrate. This objection tests the logical necessity of the author's conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full English translation. Notice the flow of the objection and the alternative proposal.
The passage is a continuous speech from the objector.
2
Identify the structure of the objection
Break the passage into two parts: the objector's critique of the author's argument, and the alternative explanation. Note the key phrases like 'we do not accept' and 'why is it not permissible'.
3
Understand the key concepts
Focus on 'quantitative nature' (الطبيعة المقدارية), 'matter' (مادة), 'substrate' (محل), and 'attributes' (صفات). These are crucial for grasping the alternative proposal.
The objector uses these terms to build a logical possibility.
4
Trace the step-by-step argument
Follow the objector's logic: (1) grant finitude but question matter, (2) reject the claim that shape implies matter, (3) propose that quantitative nature itself can be qualified, (4) conclude that dimension can dispense with matter.
The part-whole argument is used to undermine the author's inference.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection matters for the larger discussion about space. Does it refute the author? Or does it show the need for further argumentation?
The author later addresses this objection elsewhere.

ولقائل أن يقول : أما دليلكم على وجوب تناهي الأبعاد فسيأتي البحث عنه على الاستقصاء . ثم لئن سلمنا ذلك ، لكنا نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون متناهياً ؟ قوله : « إن كان متناهي فهو مشكل ، وكل مشكل فتشكله لا بد وأن يكون لأجل مادته ، قلنا : لا نسلم . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لعاد الاشتراك المذكور ، لأن مادة الجزء ، مساوية لمادة الكل . أما قوله : و سبب الشكل المعين صفات قائمة بالمادة ، أعدت تلك المادة لقبول ذلك الشكل المعين ، فنقول : فإذا عقلتم ذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : الطبيعة المقدارية وحدها من غير أن يكون لها مادة ، كانت موصوفة بصفات مخصوصة ، لأجلها صارت مستعدة لقبول ذلك الشكل المعين، وعلى هذا التقدير فتستغني الطبيعة المقدارية عن الحلول في المادة . وحينئذ يبطل قولكم : « إن كل ما كان بعداً ومقداراً ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون حالا في محل . . وليكن ههنا آخر كلامنا في نقل دلائل الطاعنين في أن المكان هو البعد . والله أعلم
And someone may say: As for your proof of the necessity of the finitude of dimensions, the investigation of it will come later comprehensively. Then even if we grant that, we would say: why is it not permissible for it to be finite? His statement: 'If it is finite then it is shaped, and every shaped thing its shaping must be due to its matter', we say: we do not accept. And if the matter were so, the aforementioned commonality would return, because the matter of the part is equal to the matter of the whole. As for his statement: 'And the cause of the particular shape is attributes subsisting in the matter, which prepared that matter to accept that particular shape', we say: since you have understood that, why is it not permissible to say: the quantitative nature alone without having matter is qualified by specific attributes, due to which it became prepared to accept that particular shape, and on this hypothesis the quantitative nature dispenses with inherence in matter. And thus your statement becomes invalid: 'Every dimension and magnitude must be inhering in a substrate.' And let this be the end of our speech in transmitting the proofs of the objectors regarding that space is the dimension. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from a critic against the author's conclusion that every dimension must inhere in matter. The objector first notes that the proof for the finitude of dimensions will be examined later, and even if granted, they do not accept that a finite dimension's shape must be due to matter. They argue that if shape were due to matter, the problem of the part and whole having the same matter would lead to identical shapes, contradicting experience. Furthermore, the objector proposes an alternative: the quantitative nature (al-tabi'a al-miqdariyya) itself could be qualified with specific attributes that make it receptive to a particular shape, without needing a material substrate. In this scenario, the dimension would be self-subsisting. The objection aims to show that the author's argument does not force the conclusion that dimension must inhere in matter. The passage ends with a note that this concludes the transmission of the arguments of the objectors concerning the view that space is the dimension.

Key Terms
طبيعة مقدارية (quantitative nature)
مادة (matter)
محل (substrate)
صفات (attributes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the objection completely refutes the author's position.
Correction: The objection merely proposes a logical alternative; the author addresses it elsewhere.

Checkpoint

B) The quantitative nature itself has specific attributes that determine shape without matter
C) The shape is determined by the will of the Giver of Forms directly
D) The dimension is eternal and unchanging

Key Takeaway
The objector suggests that a dimension's shape could originate from attributes of the quantitative nature itself, not necessarily from a material substrate.

Are you willing to entertain that God’s creative act could bypass material intermediaries entirely?
?
This passage confronts you with a logical possibility that undermines a deeply held assumption about the physical world. Where else in your life do you assume a material or natural cause simply because you cannot conceive of an alternative? How can this lesson push you to expand your understanding of God’s absolute power, and what specific step can you take today to remind yourself that His creative will is not bound by the material processes you observe?

How Form Is Received: Matter's Preparedness and the Bestower of Forms
Author answers objection about matter of whole vs. part with form reception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the necessity of matter for dimension by appealing to the preparedness of matter and the role of the Bestower of forms.

Core Idea
Matter's specific preparedness and Divine causation give it form, not matter alone.

In this passage, the author tackles a clever objection: if the matter of a whole body is the same as that of its parts, how does the whole get its unique shape? The response reveals a deeper principle — matter has qualities that make it ready to receive a particular form from the Bestower of forms. This not only answers the objection but also reinforces the author's main argument that dimension cannot exist without matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
First, read the Arabic and its translation to see the flow of the argument. Notice how the objector tries to create a symmetry problem.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for terms like mahall, madda, bu'd, shakl, and Wahib al-suwar. These are central to understanding the argument.
3
Trace the response logic
Follow how the author uses the idea of matter's preparedness (isti'dad) and the Bestower of forms to break the symmetry. Why does the whole get a different form from the part?
4
Summarize the final conclusion
Reread the last sentences: every dimension is finite, formed, and needs matter. Therefore, a pure dimension without matter is impossible.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يكون محلاً له ، ومادة له . وهو المطلوب. فإن قالوا : فمادة الكل مساوية لمادة [ الجزء (۱) ] فيعود الاشتراك المذكور . فنقول في الجواب : إن ذلك البعد لما كان حالاً في تلك المادة ، وكانت تلك المادة موصوفة بصفات ، لأجلها كانت مستعدة لقبول ذلك الشكل من واهب الصور، لا جرم حصل ذلك الشكل دون سائر الأشكال. فيثبت بمجموع ما ذكرنا : أن كل بعد فهو متناهي [ وكل متناهي (٢) ] مشكل ، وكل متشكل فإنما حصل له شكله المعين بسبب المادة ، ينتج أن كل بعد فإنه لا يوجد إلا إذا كان حاصلا في المادة . وإذا ثبت هذا كان القول بوجود البعد المجرد محالاً [ وذلك هو المطلوب (۳) ] .
That which is a locus for it and matter for it. And that is the desired conclusion. For if they say: The matter of the whole is equal to the matter of the part, so the aforementioned sharing returns. So we say in response: Since that dimension (bu'd) was inherent in that matter, and that matter was described by attributes due to which it was prepared to receive that form (shakl) from the Bestower of forms (Wahib al-suwar), inevitably that form occurred to the exclusion of other forms. Thus it is established by all that we have mentioned: that every dimension is finite, and every finite thing is formed (mutashakkil), and every formed thing only attains its specific form because of matter. It follows that every dimension does not exist unless it is present in matter. And when this is established, the statement that there exists a pure dimension (bu'd mujarrad) is impossible, and that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer cycle. The author has previously argued that every dimension must be in a locus (mahall) and have matter. An objector raises a problem: if the matter of the whole body is identical in nature to the matter of a part, then the same difficulty arises about how the whole gets its specific form. The author responds by invoking the concept of matter's preparedness (isti'dad). The dimension in question is 'inherent' (hal) in that matter, which has specific qualities that make it ready to receive a particular form from the Bestower of forms (God). Therefore, that specific shape occurs, not others. The author then summarizes: from this we deduce that every dimension is finite, every finite thing is formed, and every formed thing owes its form to matter. Hence, dimension cannot exist independently of matter; a 'pure dimension' without matter is impossible. This response avoids circularity by appealing to the divine causation of forms.

Key Terms
محلاً (locus)
مادة (matter)
بعد (dimension)
شكل (form)
واهب الصور (Bestower of forms)
متناه (finite)
متشكل (formed)
بعد مجرد (pure dimension)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That matter alone determines the form a body takes.
Correction: The author argues that matter's specific preparedness and the divine Bestower of forms are what give a body its particular shape.

Checkpoint
According to the author's response, why does the dimension's matter produce a specific form rather than any other?
A) Because matter is inherently limited to one form.
B) Because the matter is described with specific qualities that prepare it to receive that form from the Bestower of forms.
C) Because the dimension itself chooses the form.
D) Because the objector's premise is false.

Key Takeaway
Every dimension requires matter because its specific form comes from matter's preparedness and the Bestower of forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The argument rejects 'pure dimension'—meaning nothing exists without matter and a Bestower. Is there an area of your life where you act as if things run on their own, without needing Allah’s continuous creation? ?

Refuting the Surface Theory of Place
Al-Razi refutes the objection that surface theory implies stationary moving bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the surface theory of place fails to account for motion, and how the example of flour in a bag demonstrates that true place is the void or space that a body occupies.

Core Idea
Place is not the surface that touches a body, but the space it occupies.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi addresses a critical objection to the surface theory of place. The objector argues that if place were merely the inner surface of a container, then a moving object would have to be judged stationary because its surface contact remains unchanged. Al-Razi uses the simple example of flour in a bag moved from one city to another to expose the flaw and redirect the argument toward the necessity of positing void or space as the true locus of motion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's claim: if place is surface, a moving body would be stationary because surface contact remains constant. Note the flour-in-a-bag scenario.
2
Examine the 'moving by assumption' reply
The objector tries to salvage the theory by saying the flour is only moving by assumption, not intrinsically. Al-Razi finds this ambiguous.
Pay attention to how he deconstructs the phrase.
3
Follow al-Razi's refutation
He argues that the flour undeniably 'transferred in itself' from Rayy to Khurasan, even though its surface contact with the bag remained. Thus surface cannot be the real place.
4
Conclude: place is space/void
The flour moved from one region of space to another; therefore real place is the void space that a body occupies, not the containing surface.
This is the desired conclusion.

وأما قوله : إنه لو كان المكان عبارة عن السطح ، لزم أن نحكم على المتحرك حال كونه متحركاً بكونه ساكناً . فتقريره : أنا إذا وضعنا شيئاً من الدقيق في جراب . فعند القائلين بأن المكان هو السطح يكون مكان ذلك الدقيق هو السطح الباطن من ذلك الجراب ، المماس للسطح الظاهر من ذلك الدقيق . فإذا فرضنا أنه نقل ذلك الجراب من « الري » إلى « خراسان » فقد انتقل ذلك الدقيق من بلد إلى بلد ، مع أن المماسة الحاصلة بين ذلك السطحين باقية ثابتة غير متغيرة . فلو لم يكن المكان إلا السطح ، لوجب الحكم بكونه ساكناً مع أنا نعلم بالضرورة أنه انتقل من « الري » إلى « خراسان » وذلك باطل . فإن قالوا : إن ذلك الدقيق الذي في ذلك الجراب ، لا نقول : إنه متحرك بالذات ، بل نقول : إنه متحرك بالفرض . فالجواب أن نقول : إن قول القائل هذا الجسم متحرك بالفرض : لفظة مبهمة لا بد من البحث عن معناها . فنقول : لا شك أنا حين جعلنا هذا الدقيق في الجراب ، حين كنا في « الري » ، فقد كان هذا الدقيق حاصلاً في الري ، فإذا جئنا بذلك الجراب إلى « خراسان » ، فلا شك أن ذلك الدقيق حصل في « خراسان » . فذلك الدقيق الذي كان في الري ، إما أن يقال : إنه في نفسه لم ينتقل ولم يتحرك ، بل بقي كما كان . وإما أن يقال : إنه انتقل وتحرك . والأول مكابرة ، فإن هذا الدقيق كان حاصلاً في « الري » والآن حصل في « خراسان » ، فكيف يعقل أن يقال : لم ينتقل ولم يتحرك ؟ فيثبت : أنه انتقل في نفسه وتحرك في ذاته ، وظاهر أنه لم ينتقل عن ذلك السطح الذي كان محيطاً به في « الري » ، لأنه حال ما حصل في « خراسان » يقع السطح من ذلك الدقيق ، مماساً للسطح الباطن من الجراب . فعلمنا : أن المكان الحقيقي الذي يسكن فيه ذات الجسم ، ويتحرك عنه وإليه : هو الفضاء المذكور ، والخلاء الموصوف حتى يقال : إن ذات الدقيق انتقل من ذلك الفضاء الذي كان في « الري » إلى الفضاء الحاصل به في « خراسان » ، وذلك هو المطلوب.
And as for his statement: If place were the surface, then we would have to judge a moving thing, while it is moving, as being at rest. Its explanation: When we put some flour into a bag, according to those who say that place is the surface, the place of that flour is the inner surface of the bag that contacts the outer surface of that flour. If we suppose that the bag is moved from Rayy to Khurasan, then that flour has moved from one city to another, while the contact between those two surfaces remains fixed, unchanged. If place were nothing but the surface, then we would have to judge that it is at rest, while we know by necessity that it moved from Rayy to Khurasan, and that is false. If they say: We do not say that that flour in the bag is moving intrinsically, but we say it is moving by assumption. The answer is to say: The phrase 'this body is moving by assumption' is ambiguous and must be examined. We say: There is no doubt that when we put this flour into the bag while we were in Rayy, this flour existed in Rayy. When we bring that bag to Khurasan, there is no doubt that that flour has come to exist in Khurasan. That flour which was in Rayy is either said to have not moved or transferred in itself, remaining as it was, or it is said to have transferred and moved. The first is willful ignorance, for this flour existed in Rayy and now exists in Khurasan; how can it be said that it did not transfer or move? So it is established that it transferred in itself and moved in its essence, and it is apparent that it did not move away from that surface which surrounded it in Rayy, because when it came to exist in Khurasan, the surface of that flour contacts the inner surface of the bag. Thus we know that the real place in which the essence of a body resides, and from which it moves and to which it moves, is the aforementioned space and described void, so that it is said that the essence of the flour transferred from that space which was in Rayy to the space existing in Khurasan, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection against the view that place is not merely surface. The objector argues that if place were surface, a moving object would have to be judged stationary because its surface contact remains constant. The author gives the example of flour in a bag moved from Rayy to Khurasan: the inner surface of the bag remains in contact with the outer surface of the flour, yet the flour clearly moves from one city to another. So the surface theory is false. The objector then replies that the flour is not moving intrinsically but by assumption. The author responds by analyzing the phrase 'moving by assumption', arguing it is ambiguous. He points out that the flour existed in Rayy and then exists in Khurasan; denying its movement is willful ignorance. Hence the flour does move intrinsically. Moreover, it does not move away from the surface of the bag that surrounds it; rather, it moves with that surface. Therefore, the real place is not the surface but the space (void) that the body occupies, because the flour leaves one region of space and enters another. Thus the true place is the void or space, not the surface.

سطح
فضاء
خلاء
مماسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Place is merely the inner surface that touches a body.
Correction: True place is the void or space that a body occupies, as shown by the fact that a body can move while maintaining surface contact.

Checkpoint
What does the flour-in-a-bag example demonstrate about the nature of place?
A) Place is the surface of the containing body.
B) Place is the void or space that a body occupies.
C) Place is relative to the observer's perspective.
D) Place is an illusion; only motion is real.

Key Takeaway
The surface theory of place fails because a body can move while its surface contact remains constant; the real place is the space (void) through which the body moves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objector claimed the flour moved only 'by assumption' to avoid the truth. What is one area where you have used a similar excuse to avoid acknowledging an inner change that Allah is calling you to make? How will you stop hiding behind 'assumption' and accept the reality of your movement?
?
Al-Razi says denying the flour's intrinsic movement is 'willful ignorance'. What hard truth about your own spiritual state have you been denying—perhaps that you have moved away from Allah while clinging to the 'surface' of religious routine? Name one concrete step you will take today to return to the true space of your heart.

First Proof: The Self-Evident Distinction of the Two Sides of an Infinite Line
First proof that space is a self-subsisting dimension via the intuitive distinction of sides.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Matalib al-Aliyah uses the intuitive distinction between the two sides of an infinite line to prove that space is a self-subsisting dimension.

Core Idea
The two sides of an infinite line are undeniably different – this is a self-evident truth.

The author begins his proof for the reality of space by appealing to the most basic level of human cognition: self-evident truths. He presents a thought experiment involving an infinite line, the axis of the universe, and asks us to consider its two sides. Without any reasoning, we immediately know they are distinct. Denying this, he claims, is to deny the strongest self-evident truths.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noticing its concise structure. Let the author's claim settle.
The passage is a single argument, not a series of steps.
2
Understand the thought experiment
Imagine an infinite line as the axis of the universe. Visualize its two sides extending to infinity. Ask yourself: are the two sides different or the same?
This is a thought experiment to elicit a self-evident judgment.
3
Reflect on the epistemological claim
The author says this distinction is known by 'badahat our intellects' (the intuition of our intellects). Consider what this means for the proof's foundation.
This appeal to self-evidence is a key method in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنا ببداهة عقولنا نعلم أن الخط الذي هو محور العالم ، لو خرج من جانبيه إلى ما لا نهاية له ، فإن أحد جانبي ذلك الخط ، مغاير للجانب الآخر منه . ومن أنكر هذا التغاير ، فقد نازع في أقوى البديهيات
First: By the intuition of our intellects we know that the line that is the axis of the universe, if it extends from its two sides to infinity, then one side of that line is different from the other side. Whoever denies this difference has disputed the strongest of self-evident truths.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that space (al-makan) is a self-subsisting dimension (al-bu'd al-qa'im bi-dhatihi) rather than a containing surface (al-sath al-hawi). The proof proceeds by appealing to a self-evident judgment: take an imaginary infinite line (the axis of the universe). Its two sides are intuitively distinct. If someone denies this distinction, they are denying one of the most obvious self-evident truths (al-badihiyyat). The strength of this proof lies in its reliance on immediate, intuitive knowledge that cannot be doubted.

Key Terms

الخط
محور العالم
البديهيات
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the distinction of sides is merely conventional or a product of imagination.
Correction: The author insists it is a self-evident truth of the intellect, known immediately without reasoning.

Checkpoint

A) It is a matter of opinion
B) It is a self-evident truth of the intellect
C) It is a product of imagination
D) It is learned through reasoning

Key Takeaway
The intuitive distinction between the two sides of an infinite line is a self-evident truth that serves as a foundational proof for the objective reality of space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author says denying such a truth is ‘disputing the strongest of self-evident truths.’ Is there a truth you claim to believe but live as if it were false? Name it, and decide on one concrete step to live in accordance with it this week.

Second Proof: The Necessary Distinction of Front and Back at the North Pole
Second proof that space is a self-subsisting dimension using the necessary distinction of front and back at the North Pole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessary distinction of front and back at the North Pole provides a self-evident proof for space as a self-subsisting dimension.

Core Idea
Front and back are known necessarily, even at the North Pole — proving space is self-subsisting.

This second proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah uses a simple thought experiment. Imagine a person standing exactly at the North Pole, where all conventional directions converge. Despite this, the person still has an undeniable awareness of front and back. This necessary knowledge shows that spatial distinctions are not dependent on external landmarks but are part of a fundamental, self-subsisting dimension. In this lesson, we will unpack this concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the key terms: 'قطب شمالي' (North Pole), 'امتياز' (distinction), and 'ضروري' (necessary).
2
Understand the thought experiment
Visualize a person at the North Pole. Why must they still distinguish front from back? Think about the necessity of this distinction regardless of surroundings.
The example shows that spatial awareness is intrinsic.
3
Grasp the logical inference
Reflect on why this proves space is self-subsisting. The necessary distinction cannot rely on external objects; it points to a foundational dimension.
This is a key argument for the nature of space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنا لو فرضنا إنساناً واقفاً على طرف القطب الشمالي ، فإنه لا بد وأن يميز قدامه عن خلفه . والعلم بهذا الامتياز ضروري
Second: If we suppose a man standing at the tip of the North Pole, he must necessarily distinguish his front from his back. Knowledge of this distinction is necessary.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof. Imagine a person standing exactly at the North Pole. There, all directions (north, south, east, west) converge, yet the person still has an intrinsic front and back. The distinction between front and back is a necessary and immediate knowledge. This shows that the sense of spatial distinction is not dependent on external landmarks but is a fundamental, self-evident notion. It further supports the claim that space is a self-subsisting dimension.

Key Takeaway
The necessary and self-evident distinction between front and back, even at the North Pole, demonstrates that space is a self-subsisting dimension independent of external objects.

Third Proof: Movement of a Point on a Rotating Sphere
Third proof that space is a self-subsisting dimension using point movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the movement of a point on a rotating sphere proves that space is a self-subsisting dimension.

Core Idea
Movement is transfer from one locus to another, proving space is a dimension.

This third proof uses a simple observation: a point on a rotating sphere moves. What does movement mean? The author argues it is nothing but a transfer from one locus to another, implying the existence of distinct loci. These loci are self-subsisting dimensions, establishing space as a dimension rather than a surface.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic then the translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, definition, conclusion.
2
Understand the definition of movement
The passage defines movement as the transfer from one locus to another. Why is this definition important?
3
Identify the implication
If movement requires distinct loci, then these loci must be real entities. What does that tell us about space?
4
Connect to the overall argument
This proof supports the claim that space is a self-subsisting dimension. Consider how this builds on previous proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن النقطة المفروضة في السطح الأعلى من الفلك ، لا شك أنها تكون متحركة حال استدارة الفلك ، ولا معنى لتلك الحركة ، إلا أن تلك النقطة انتقلت من حيز إلى حيز آخر وذلك هو المطلوب
Third: The point assumed on the upper surface of the sphere is undoubtedly moving during the rotation of the sphere, and there is no meaning to that movement except that that point has transferred from one locus to another locus, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. Consider a point on the celestial sphere; as the sphere rotates, the point moves. Movement is defined as the transfer from one locus (hayyiz) to another. This transfer implies the existence of distinct loci, which are nothing other than self-subsisting dimensions (al-ab'ad al-qa'imah bi-dhatiha). Thus, space must be a dimension, not a surface, because the point moves through successive positions that are themselves dimensions.

Commit to one moment today where you consciously acknowledge Allah’s preservation of the space you are in.

Refutation of the Illusion Objection with Three Arguments
Refutation of the objection that the judgment about space is from illusion, with three counter-arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments refuting the claim that the judgment about space is from illusion, and why these arguments establish that space is a self-subsisting dimension.

Core Idea
Three arguments show that the certainty about space cannot be from illusion without collapsing all knowledge.

The objection claims that our judgment about space is a product of imagination and therefore false. The author refutes this with three powerful arguments rooted in epistemology: absolute certainty, the self-evident nature of the judgment, and the impossibility of systematic doubt. Let us examine each argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
First argument: absolute certainty
Read the first argument from the Arabic. The author states that the certainty in the judgment is absolute and indistinguishable from other self-evident truths. If such certainty could be false, all self-evident truths become suspect, leading to circularity because inference depends on them.
Notice the use of 'دور' (circularity) as a key logical flaw.
2
Second argument: self-evident vs. inferential knowledge
Read the second argument. The objector arbitrarily divides judgments into those from intellect (truthful) and those from imagination (false). To distinguish them requires additional knowledge, which either is self-evident (contradicting the objector's claim) or inferential (requiring self-evident truths, causing circularity and sophistry).
The term 'سفسطة' (sophistry) appears here as the consequence.
3
Third argument: undoubtability
Read the third argument. A self-evident truth is something a rational person cannot doubt. The distinction between north and south poles is such a proposition, so it is self-evident. The author concludes that space is a self-subsisting dimension, not a containing surface.
Note the example of the poles to illustrate the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : ( إن هذا الحكم عمل الوهم والخيال . وذلك كاذب ) . فنقول : هذا الكلام باطل من وجوه : الأول : وهو أنا نجد الجزم بصحة هذه القضية جزماً قاطعاً غير قابل للشك والشبهة ، ولا نجد في القلب تفاوتاً بين قوة هذه القضية وبين سائر البديهيات . وإذا كان كذلك فنقول : لو جوزنا أن تكون القضية مع هذا القدر من القوة كاذبة فحينئذ يرتفع الأمان عن البديهيات ، ويحتاج في تصحيح ما يصح منها إلى النظر والاستدلال ، لكن الاستدلال موقوف على صحة البديهيات ، فيلزم الدور . وهو فاسد . والثاني : وهو إنا نجد في فطرة النفس السليمة عن الآفات ، وغريزة القلب المبرأة عن المشوشات أحكاماً يقينية قاطعة، ثم أنتم تقولون : الحاكم ببعضها هو العقل ، وهو صادق والحاكم بالبعض الآخر هو الوهم وهو كاذب . فعلى هذا التقرير ما لم يُعرف الفرق بين العقل وبين الوهم ، وما لم يُعرف ثانياً أن هذا الحكم هو حكم العقل لا حكم الوهم ، وما لم يُعرف ثالثاً أن العقل صادق ، وأن الوهم كاذب . فإنه لا يمكننا أن نحكم بصحة تلك القضية التي جزمنا بصحتها . ونقول : العلم بهذه الأمور الثلاثة إن كان بديهياً ، امتنع حصول الجزم في القضايا الكاذبة ، مع حصول العلم البديهي بكونه كاذباً . وإن كان استدلالياً لزم توقف البديهيات على الدلائل لكن الدلائل موقوفة على البديهيات . فيلزم الدور وتبطل كل العلوم وتلزم السفسطة . الثالث : وهو أنه لا معنى للعلوم البديهية الأولية إلا القضايا التي أراد العاقل تشكيل نفسه فيها لما قدر عليه لكن العلم بأنه لا بد وأن يمتاز جانب القطب الشمالي عن جانب القطب الجنوبي، علم لو أراد العاقل تشكيك نفسه فيه لما قدر عليه فوجب أن يكون ذلك من العلوم البديهية . فهذا مجموع الدلائل الدالة على أن المكان عبارة عن البعد القائم بذاته ، لا عن السطح الحاوي . والله أعلم.
As for his statement: 'This judgment is the work of imagination (al-wahm) and illusion (al-khayal), and that is false.' We say: This statement is invalid from several aspects: First: We find that the certainty in the correctness of this proposition is an absolute certainty, not subject to doubt or ambiguity, and we find no difference in the heart between the strength of this proposition and other self-evident truths. If that is so, we say: If we allowed that a proposition with this degree of strength could be false, then trust in self-evident truths would be lifted, and one would need reflection and inference to correct what is correct among them. But inference depends on the validity of self-evident truths, so circularity follows. And that is invalid. Second: We find in the pure nature of the soul free from defects, and the instinct of the heart cleared of disturbances, certain and definitive judgments. Then you say: The judge of some of them is the intellect, and it is truthful; the judge of others is the imagination, and it is false. On this account, unless the difference between intellect and imagination is known, and unless it is secondly known that this judgment is a judgment of the intellect and not of the imagination, and unless it is thirdly known that the intellect is truthful and the imagination is false, we cannot judge the correctness of that proposition which we are certain of. We say: Knowledge of these three matters, if it is self-evident, it would be impossible to have certainty in false propositions while having self-evident knowledge that they are false. And if it is inferential, then self-evident truths would depend on proofs, but proofs depend on self-evident truths, so circularity follows, all sciences become invalid, and sophistry follows. Third: There is no meaning to primary self-evident sciences except those propositions that a rational person, if he tried to doubt himself in them, would not be able to do so. The knowledge that the side of the North Pole must be distinct from the side of the South Pole is knowledge that if a rational person tried to doubt himself in it, he would not be able to. Therefore, it must be among the self-evident sciences. This is the totality of the proofs indicating that space (al-makan) is a self-subsisting dimension (al-bu'd al-qa'im bi-dhatihi), not a containing surface (al-sath al-hawi). And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection claiming that the judgment about space is a product of imagination (wahm) and illusion (khayal), and therefore false. The author presents three counter-arguments. First: The judgment is held with absolute certainty, indistinguishable from other self-evident truths; if we allowed such certainty to be false, all self-evident truths would become suspect, leading to circularity because inference depends on them. Second: Our minds possess certain judgments, but the objector arbitrarily attributes some to intellect (truthful) and others to imagination (false); to know which judgment belongs to which faculty requires additional knowledge, which itself is either self-evident (but then the judgment in question would be self-evident) or inferential (leading again to circularity). Third: A self-evident truth is defined as a proposition that a rational person cannot doubt; the distinction between the north and south poles is such a proposition, so it is self-evident. The passage concludes that all these proofs demonstrate that space is a self-subsisting dimension, not a containing surface.

Key Terms
وهم
خيال
بديهة
دور
سفسطة
عقل
فطرة
غريزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that self-evident truths can be doubted or are subjective.
Correction: The author argues that doubting them leads to sophistry and the collapse of all knowledge; they are foundational and indubitable.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against the claim that the judgment about space is from imagination?
A) The judgment is based on religious texts
B) The judgment is held with absolute certainty like other self-evident truths
C) The judgment is purely empirical
D) The judgment is agreed upon by all scholars

Key Takeaway
The passage establishes that space is a self-subsisting dimension (al-bu'd al-qa'im bi-dhatihi) by showing that the judgment about space is self-evident and cannot be dismissed as illusory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever allowed a sophisticated intellectual objection to shake your certainty about something as basic as the reality of space or your own existence? What does this passage say about the danger of such doubt, and how will you guard against it from today?
?
The author argues that doubt in self-evident truths leads to sophistry—the loss of all knowledge. Is there a self-evident truth about Allah or His creation that you have hesitantly doubted? Will you commit to treating that doubt as the first step toward sophistry and reject it?
?
Consider the distinction between intellect and imagination in your own life. When have you allowed imagination (fear, desire, assumption) to override the clear judgment of your intellect? How does this passage call you to trust your rational nature more fully, especially in matters of faith?

The First Proof for the Possibility of Empty Space
First proof for the possibility of empty space with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the possibility of empty space, including how Sanusi avoids circularity and addresses an objection about substance and accident.

Core Idea
Empty space is possible because space and body cannot depend on each other.

Sanusi introduces the debate on empty space and presents his first proof based on the independence of space from body. He then addresses an objection concerning the relationship between substance and accident, clarifying which accidents are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
Read the Arabic and English translation. Notice that Aristotle holds empty space impossible, while the majority of theologians and early philosophers hold it possible. Sanusi chooses the latter.
2
Understand the first proof's logic
The proof uses reason: body depends on place. If place also depended on body, there would be a circular dependency, which is absurd. Therefore place is independent of body, so it can exist without body.
This is a classic kalam argument against circularity.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
The objector says the same reasoning would apply to substance and accident, making body impossible to exist without accident, yet body must have accidents. Sanusi answers by distinguishing necessary accidents (like spatial location) from separable ones (like color).
Necessary accidents are like effects of the body's essence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that spatial occupancy is a necessary accident, so body must occupy space. But space itself is independent of body, so it can exist empty. This establishes the possibility of empty space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في بيان أن الخلاء ممتنع الوقوع أو ممكن الوقوع؟ مذهب أرسطاطاليس: أنه ممتنع الوقوع. وبه قال جميع أتباعه. ومذهب جمهور الأقدمين من الحكماء، وجمهور المتكلمين: أنه جائز الوقوع. وهو المختار. ويدل عليه وجوه: الحجة الأولى: إن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن الجسم الذي هو المتمكن مفتقر إلى الفضاء الذي هو المكان، فلو كان وجود الفضاء الذي هو المكان ممتنع الوجود إلا مع المتمكن، لزم افتقار كل واحد منها إلى الآخر، فيكون دوراً، وهو محال. فيثبت: أن الفضاء غني في وجوده وفي ذاته عن المتمكن. وهو أيضاً غير موجب لذات المتمكن وإلا لامتنع أن يفارق المتمكن. فيثبت: أن المكان غني في ذاته عن المتمكن، وغير موجب له، وما كان كذلك لم يمتنع منفكاً عنه، فيلزم القطع بأنه لا يمتنع وجود الحيز والفضاء خالياً عن المتمكن. ولا معنى للخلاء إلا ذلك. فإن قيل: يشكل ما ذكرتم بالجوهر والعرض. فإن كل واحد منهما غني في وجوده عن الآخر. وإلا وقع الدور، وأحدهما غير موجب للآخر، وإلا لامتنع وجود الجسم بدون ذلك العرض، مع أنه يمتنع حصول الجسم إلا مع العرض. والجواب: الأعراض عندنا على قسمين: منها ما يجري مجرى المعلول لذات الجسم، ومنها ما لا يكون كذلك. أما القسم الأول: فهو كالحصول في الحيز. فإن ماهية الجسم تقتضي كونها حاصلة في الحيز، فلا جرم ممتنع خلو ذات الجسم عن الحصول في الحيز. ثم إن كان ذلك الحصول حاصلاً عقيب حصول آخر، فهو الحركة. وإن لم يكن كذلك فهو السكون. وفي الحقيقة: يرجع حاصل القول في الحركة والسكون إلى الحصول في الحيز، لكنا بينا أن الحصول في الحيز كالمعلول لماهية الجسم، وكالموجب عنها، فلا جرم قلنا: إنه يمتنع خلو ذات الجسم عن الحركة والسكون، وأما سائر الأعراض فهي كالألوان والطعوم والروائح وغيرها، وعندنا يجوز خلو الجسم عنها. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لا شك أن الجسم مفتقر إلى الحيز، فيمتنع كون الجسم علة الحصول الحيز وأيضاً: الحيز يمتنع كونه علة لوجود الجسم. وإذا ثبت هذا ظهر أنه ليس واحد منهما علة الحصول الآخر، فلما ثبت أن الجسم محتاج إلى الحيز، وجب كون الحيز غنياً عن الجسم، فوجب جواز حصول الحيز خالياً عن الجسم. وهذا بخلاف الجسم مع الحصول في الحيز فإنا بينا: أن ماهية الجسم علة للحصول في الحيز فظهر الفرق. وأما سائر الأعراض فقد ذكرنا أنه لا يمتنع خلو الجسم عنها بأسرها. فقد زال النقض. الحجة الثانية على صحة القول بالخلاء: أنه لو امتنع الحيز عن المتمكن، لكان إمساك الحاصل أسهل من تحصيل المفقود، فكان يلزم أن لا يمكن الحيز من خروج المتمكن منه، فكان يلزم أن يمتنع على الجسم أن يتحرك عن مكانه، وحيث لم يمتنع، علمنا أن ذلك لأجل أن ذات الحيز ممكن.
Chapter Five on Explaining whether Empty Space is Impossible to Occur or Possible to Occur? The doctrine of Aristotle: that it is impossible to occur. And all his followers said the same. And the doctrine of the majority of the early philosophers, and the majority of the theologians: that it is possible to occur. And it is the chosen view. And it is indicated by proofs: The first proof: The intuition of reason judges that the body, which is the occupant, is dependent on the void, which is place. So if the existence of the void, which is place, were impossible except with the occupant, then each of them would be dependent on the other, resulting in a circle, and that is impossible. Thus it is established that the void is independent in its existence and in its essence from the occupant. And it is also not a necessitator of the essence of the occupant, otherwise it would be impossible for it to separate from the occupant. Thus it is established that place is independent in its essence from the occupant, and not a necessitator of it, and whatever is like that is not impossible to be separate from it. Therefore it necessarily follows that it is not impossible for the dimension (al-hayyiz) and the void to exist empty of the occupant. And there is no meaning of emptiness except that. If it is said: What you have mentioned is problematic with the substance and the accident. For each of them is independent in its existence from the other, otherwise a circle would occur, and one of them is not a necessitator of the other, otherwise it would be impossible for the body to exist without that accident, while the body cannot occur except with an accident. And the answer: Accidents according to us are of two types: some are like an effect (ma'lul) of the essence of the body, and some are not like that. As for the first type: it is like occupying a dimension. For the essence of the body necessitates that it occupies a dimension, so it is impossible for the essence of the body to be empty of occupying a dimension. Then if that occupancy occurs after another occupancy, it is motion. And if it is not like that, it is rest. And in reality, the sum of the statement regarding motion and rest reduces to occupying a dimension, but we have shown that occupying a dimension is like an effect of the essence of the body, and like a necessitator from it, so we say: it is impossible for the essence of the body to be empty of motion and rest. As for other accidents, such as colors, tastes, smells, and others, according to us it is permissible for the body to be empty of them. When this is established, we say: There is no doubt that the body is dependent on the dimension, so it is impossible for the body to be the cause of the occupation of the dimension. Also, it is impossible for the dimension to be the cause of the existence of the body. And when this is established, it appears that neither of them is the cause of the other. Since it is established that the body needs the dimension, the dimension must be independent of the body, so it must be permissible for the dimension to occur empty of the body. This is different from the body with the occupation of the dimension, for we have shown that the essence of the body is the cause of occupying the dimension, so the difference appears. As for other accidents, we have mentioned that it is not impossible for the body to be empty of all of them. Thus the objection is removed. The second proof for the soundness of the doctrine of emptiness: that if the dimension were impossible without the occupant, then holding what is present would be easier than obtaining what is missing, so it would follow that the dimension cannot allow the occupant to leave it, so it would follow that it would be impossible for the body to move from its place. And since it is not impossible, we know that this is because the essence of the dimension is possible.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a theological-philosophical argument on the possibility of empty space (al-khala'). The author begins by stating the two positions: Aristotle and his followers hold that empty space is impossible, while the majority of early philosophers and theologians hold that it is possible. The author chooses the latter. He then presents two proofs. The first proof argues from the independence of space from body: reason judges that a body depends on place, but if place also depended on body, there would be a circular dependency, which is absurd. Therefore place must be independent of body in its existence and essence, and not a necessitator of the body. Hence it is not impossible for place to exist without a body—this is the meaning of empty space. An objection is raised: the same reasoning would apply to substance and accident—each is independent of the other without circularity, yet the body cannot exist without accidents. The author answers by distinguishing two types of accidents: some, like spatial occupancy, are necessary effects of the body's essence and thus inseparable; others, like color and taste, are separable. Since spatial occupancy is an effect of the body, the body cannot exist without it, but this does not imply that space itself is dependent on the body. The first proof concludes that space is independent and thus can exist without body. The second proof is a reductio: if space were impossible without an occupant, then retaining an occupant would be easier than obtaining one, and space would not allow the occupant to leave, so bodies could not move. But they do move, so the premise is false; hence empty space is possible.
