Causality and Created Things
intermediate
10 min
Second Proof Against Void: Body Cannot Rest or Move in It
Fakhr al-Din al-Razi argues that body cannot be at rest or in motion in a void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a body cannot be at rest or in motion in a void, and how this leads to the conclusion that void cannot serve as a place for body.

Core Idea
If void existed, rest and motion become impossible due to the absence of distinguishing factors.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's second argument against the void is a logical masterpiece: he shows that whether void is absolute nothingness or uniform extension, its parts are indistinguishable. Therefore, a body cannot have a reason to be at rest in one part rather than another, nor can it have a reason to move from one part to another. The argument is reinforced by addressing the objection that a free agent could arbitrarily choose a location. This lesson unpacks the reasoning that ultimately disproves the possibility of void.

Guided Path
1
Read the full argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the clear structure: initial claim, analysis of void, argument against rest, argument against motion, objection and response.
Pay attention to the phrase 'tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ' — preference without a preponderator.
2
Understand the nature of void
Al-Razi considers two definitions of void: pure nothingness (mutakallimūn) or uniform extension (philosophers). In both cases, void has no internal differences.
This uniformity is key to the argument.
3
Follow the argument against rest
If a body rests in a specific part of void, it must have a reason. Since all parts are identical, there is no reason — so rest is impossible.
This is an application of the principle of sufficient reason.
4
Follow the argument against motion
Motion requires leaving one part for another. If all parts are identical, there is no reason to leave one and seek another. Thus motion is also impossible.
Hence void cannot be a place for either rest or motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إنه لو حصل الخلاء ، لكان كون الجسم ساكناً فيه : محالاً ، ولكان كونه متحركاً فيه : محالاً . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع كون الخلاء مكاناً للجسم. إنما قلنا : إن الخلاء يمتنع كونه مكاناً للجسم المتحرك وللجسم الساكن . وذلك لأن الخلاء إما أن يكون عدماً محضاً ، ونفياً صرفاً، على ما يقوله المتكلمون . أو يكون بعداً متشابه الأجزاء والجوانب على ما هو قول القدماء من الحكماء. وعلى التقديرين فلا اختلاف بين أجزائه وأبعاضه البتة ، وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع أن يسكن الجسم في جزء من أجزائه وجانب من جوانبه . لأن الجسم لو بقي مستقراً فيه طالباً له لازماً للبقاء فيه مع كونه مساوياً لسائر الجوانب والأطراف، يلزم رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح، وهو محال .
فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الفاعل المختار يخصص ذلك بذلك الحيز المعين بقصده واختياره ؟ فنقول : إن كان ذلك الحيز المعين موصوفاً بأمر، لأجله صار أولى بتخصيص ذلك الجسم به، عاد الطلب في أن ذلك الحيز، كيف اختص بذلك المرجح ؟ وإن لم يوجد هذا المرجح، كان رجحان القصد إلى تخصيص ذلك الجسم به دون سائر الجوانب ترجيحاً للممكن من غير مرجح . وأيضاً فيمتنع أن يتحرك الجسم فيه . لأن الحركة عبارة عن ترك حيز، وطلب حيز آخر . وذلك لا يتم إلا بأن يكون ذلك الحيز مخالفاً لذلك الحيز، في أمر من الأمور، وإلا لكان تخصيص أحد الجانبين بالهرب، والآخر بالطلب، ترجيحاً للممكن من غير مرجح . وهو محال . فثبت : أن بتقدير حصول الخلاء يمتنع كون الجسم ساكناً فيه، ويمتنع كونه متحركاً فيه . وإذا كان كذلك، وجب القطع بأنه يمتنع أن يكون الخلاء مكاناً للجسم . والله أعلم .
The second proof: If void existed, then for a body to be at rest in it would be impossible, and for it to be in motion in it would be impossible. And whenever the matter is thus, void's being a place for body is impossible. We only say: void is impossible as a place for the moving body and for the stationary body. That is because void is either pure non-existence and absolute negation, as the theologians (mutakallimūn) say, or an extension similar in parts and aspects, as is the saying of the ancient philosophers. On both suppositions, there is no difference between its parts and portions at all. And if it is thus, it is impossible for the body to be stationary in a part of its parts and a side of its sides. Because if the body remained settled in it, seeking it, adhering to remaining in it, while it is equal to all other sides and extremities, it would necessitate preference of one of the two possible sides over the other without a preponderator, and that is impossible. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that the free agent (al-fā‘il al-mukhtār) specifies that body to that specific location by his intention and choice? We say: If that specific location is described with something due to which it becomes more worthy of specifying that body with it, then the inquiry returns as to how that location became distinguished by that preponderator. And if this preponderator does not exist, then the preference of the intention to specify that body with it over all other sides would be a preponderance of the possible without a preponderator. And also, it is impossible for the body to move in it. Because motion is leaving one location and seeking another location. That is not accomplished unless that location is different from that location in some matter; otherwise, specifying one side by fleeing and the other by seeking would be a preponderance of the possible without a preponderator, and that is impossible. So it is established that, given the existence of void, it is impossible for the body to be at rest in it, and it is impossible for it to be in motion in it. And if it is thus, it is necessary to conclude that it is impossible for void to be a place for body. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the existence of void (al-khalāʼ). The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, begins by stating the conclusion: if void existed, a body could neither be at rest nor in motion in it, so void cannot be a place for body. He then justifies this by analyzing the nature of void. Void, he explains, is either absolute nothingness (as the mutakallimūn hold) or an extension uniform in all parts (as the ancient philosophers hold). In either case, its parts are indistinguishable. Therefore, if a body were at rest in a particular part of void, there would be no reason for it to prefer that part over any other—this would be a preference without a preponderator (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ), which is impossible. Likewise, motion requires that the body leaves one location and seeks another; but if all parts of void are identical, there is no reason to flee one and seek another, again implying preference without cause. The author then anticipates an objection: could not a free agent (God) simply choose a specific location? The response is that if the location has no distinguishing feature, the agent's choice would still be arbitrary preference without a reason. If it does have a distinguishing feature, then that feature becomes the preponderator, and the question shifts to how that feature itself was determined. Thus, the argument stands: void cannot serve as a place, and since a body must be in some place, void cannot exist.

Key Terms
الخلاء
السكون
الحركة
المرجح
الفاعل المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's arbitrary choice can determine a body's location in void without a reason.
Correction: The argument shows that even if a free agent chooses, the choice itself would be a preference without a preponderator if the location has no distinguishing feature, which is rationally impossible.

Checkpoint
Why is a body's rest in void considered impossible according to the second argument?
A) Because void does not exist at all.
B) Because if all parts of void are identical, preferring one part for rest would be a preference without a preponderator.
C) Because bodies cannot be at rest in any place.
D) Because the mutakallimūn deny the existence of void.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of rest and motion in void proves that void cannot serve as a place for body, and thus void cannot exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make decisions, do you often rely on arbitrary preferences or do you seek legitimate reasons? How does the argument about 'preference without a preponderator' challenge you to examine the grounds of your choices?
?
The objection suggests that God's arbitrary choice could explain the body's location, but the argument shows even divine will requires a reason. Do you live as if your actions are guided by wisdom or by whim? What would change if you held every decision accountable to a reason?
?
The argument demonstrates that something cannot be a place if it provides no basis for rest or motion. In your own life, are there 'voids'—areas where you expect stability or progress but lack clear reasons? How can you fill those voids with purpose?
